<w:tcPr><w:tcW w:w="8360" w:type="dxa"/><w:tcBorders><w:top w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:left w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:bottom w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:right w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/></w:tcBorders><w:shd w:val="clear" w:color="auto" w:fill="1A3A5C"/><w:tcMar><w:top w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:left w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/><w:bottom w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:right w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/></w:tcMar><w:vAlign w:val="center"/></w:tcPr><w:p w14:paraId="77143468" w14:textId="77777777" w:rsidR="00814D14" w:rsidRDefault="00000000"><w:pPr><w:spacing w:line="320" w:lineRule="auto"/></w:pPr><w:r><w:rPr><w:b/><w:bCs/><w:color w:val="FFFFFF"/><w:sz w:val="28"/><w:szCs w:val="28"/></w:rPr><w:t>Executive Summary
FoodCost
 is a multi-tenant SaaS platform designed to give restaurant owners and food-truck operators complete visibility into their food costs, margins, and purchasing decisions. The system is built as a fully containerised, production-ready application from day one, with a Go API backend, a Next.js 16 frontend, PostgreSQL as the primary data store, Redis for caching and job queuing, and 
Asynq
 for asynchronous workloads.
Core Value Proposition
Food cost management is one of the highest-leverage activities in any food-service business. A 1% reduction in food cost percentage on a $1M revenue operation saves $10,000 annually. 
FoodCost
 gives operators the data layer to achieve and maintain that improvement by:
Calculating the exact cost of every recipe and menu item in real time.
Tracking inventory with full movement history and theoretical vs. actual variance.
Surfacing pricing suggestions and menu engineering insights.
Managing supplier relationships and purchase orders in one place.
Delivering detailed analytics with CSV export and trend history.
Confirmed Scope (v1)

<w:tcPr><w:tcW w:w="8360" w:type="dxa"/><w:tcBorders><w:top w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:left w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:bottom w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:right w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/></w:tcBorders><w:shd w:val="clear" w:color="auto" w:fill="1A3A5C"/><w:tcMar><w:top w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:left w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/><w:bottom w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:right w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/></w:tcMar><w:vAlign w:val="center"/></w:tcPr><w:p w14:paraId="78055123" w14:textId="77777777" w:rsidR="00814D14" w:rsidRDefault="00000000"><w:pPr><w:spacing w:line="320" w:lineRule="auto"/></w:pPr><w:r><w:rPr><w:b/><w:bCs/><w:color w:val="FFFFFF"/><w:sz w:val="28"/><w:szCs w:val="28"/></w:rPr><w:t>Architecture Overview
High-Level Design Principles
Single responsibility per service — no business logic in the proxy, no DB calls in the frontend.
Tenant isolation at the database layer via PostgreSQL Row-Level Security — not just application checks.
All blocking I/O (email, webhooks, large exports, CSV processing) is offloaded to the async worker.
One Go binary serves dual roles (API and worker) via a startup flag, keeping the image count minimal.
Redis holds only ephemeral data: active access token sessions, magic link tokens, email verification tokens, password reset tokens, and the 
Asynq
 queue broker. All durable auth state (refresh tokens, user records) lives in PostgreSQL.
PostgreSQL is the single source of truth for all persistent state.

Asynq
 queue monitoring
Request Flow
Every inbound request 
travels
 through the following layers:
Client (browser / mobile) initiates HTTPS request.
Traefik
 terminates TLS and routes: path prefix /
api
/* → go-api:8080, all other paths → web:3000.
Go API middleware chain runs: JWT validation → tenant extraction (sets 
app.org_id
 in Postgres session) → role check → plan gate.
Domain handler executes business logic, calls store layer.
Store layer runs parameterised queries against PostgreSQL. RLS policies enforce tenant isolation transparently.
Response serialised as JSON, returned through 
Traefik
 to client.

All queries use 
pgx
 named parameters. Tenant scoping is a belt-and-suspenders arrangement: the JWT claim carries 
org_id
 and the Postgres session variable enforces it at the DB level.

<w:tcPr><w:tcW w:w="8360" w:type="dxa"/><w:tcBorders><w:top w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:left w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:bottom w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:right w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/></w:tcBorders><w:shd w:val="clear" w:color="auto" w:fill="1A3A5C"/><w:tcMar><w:top w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:left w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/><w:bottom w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:right w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/></w:tcMar><w:vAlign w:val="center"/></w:tcPr><w:p w14:paraId="1A16A92F" w14:textId="77777777" w:rsidR="00814D14" w:rsidRDefault="00000000"><w:pPr><w:spacing w:line="320" w:lineRule="auto"/></w:pPr><w:r><w:rPr><w:b/><w:bCs/><w:color w:val="FFFFFF"/><w:sz w:val="28"/><w:szCs w:val="28"/></w:rPr><w:t>Data Model
Tenant Hierarchy
The entire data model is anchored on a four-level ownership hierarchy. Every table below Venue carries both 
org_id
 and 
venue_id
. PostgreSQL RLS policies enforce tenant isolation: the Go API sets SET LOCAL 
app.org_id
 = $1 at the start of every transaction, and every policy checks 
current_setting
('
app.org_id
').

  └
─  
OrgMembership
  (role: owner | admin | viewer)
         └
─  Organisation
  ← billing subject
                └
─  Venue
  ← operational unit
                       ├
─  Ingredient
         ├

  (→ 
RecipeIngredient
)
                       ├
─

           ├
─

                       ├
─

  ├
─  Supplier
                       └
─

  (→

)

4.1  Authentication
 & Identity
Authentication supports three mechanisms: email + password (primary), magic links (
passwordless
), and OAuth (social login). All three converge on the same token issuance pipeline — a short-lived JWT access token plus a long-lived refresh token stored in PostgreSQL.

<w:tcPr><w:tcW w:w="9160" w:type="dxa"/><w:tcBorders><w:top w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:left w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:bottom w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:right w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/></w:tcBorders><w:shd w:val="clear" w:color="auto" w:fill="EBF3FB"/><w:tcMar><w:top w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:left w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/><w:bottom w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:right w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/></w:tcMar><w:vAlign w:val="center"/></w:tcPr><w:p w14:paraId="42261FC1" w14:textId="77777777" w:rsidR="00814D14" w:rsidRDefault="00000000"><w:pPr><w:spacing w:line="300" w:lineRule="auto"/></w:pPr><w:r><w:rPr><w:color w:val="222222"/><w:sz w:val="21"/><w:szCs w:val="21"/></w:rPr><w:t xml:space="preserve">Access token sessions are stored in Redis (fast lookup, automatic TTL-based expiry). Refresh tokens are stored in PostgreSQL (durable, revocable, 
queryable
 for security audits). These are two distinct stores with two distinct responsibilities.

Redis Session Schema
Every live access token has a matching session record in Redis. This is the authoritative revocation check — a token whose session has been deleted or has 
revoked_at
 set is immediately invalid even if the JWT signature is still valid.

Session creation timestamp.
PostgreSQL Refresh Token Table
Refresh tokens live in PostgreSQL so they survive Redis restarts, can be listed per device, and can be 
bulk-revoked
. The raw token 
is never persisted
 server-side.

Email + Password Flow
User submits POST /auth/login with 
{ email
, 
password }
.
API loads User by email. If not found → return 401 with a generic message (no user enumeration).
API verifies the submitted password against the stored 
bcrypt
 hash (cost factor 12). Mismatch → 401.
If account email is not verified → 403 with error code 
email_not_verified
.
On success: create Redis session, issue signed 15-min JWT (
session_id
 in claims), insert 
refresh_tokens
 row in Postgres, return JWT in response body + refresh token in 
httpOnly
 cookie.
Registration Flow
User submits POST /auth/register with 
{ email
, password, 
display_
name
 }
.
API 
validates:
 email format, password strength (min 8 chars, at least 1 uppercase, 1 digit, 1 special character).
If email already exists → 409 Conflict with generic wording (no enumeration).
API hashes password with 
bcrypt
 (cost 12) and inserts User with 
email_verified
 = false.
Asynq
 task 
auth:send
_verification_email
 dispatched. Worker sends signed verification link (token SHA-256 hashed in Redis, TTL 24h, single-use).
User cannot log in until the verification link is clicked (enforced in the login flow at step 4 above).
Magic Link Flow
User submits POST /auth/magic with 
{ email
 }.
API always returns 200 OK regardless of whether the email exists, to prevent enumeration.
If the account exists: generate a cryptographically random 32-byte token, store SHA-256(token) in Redis under key 
magic:{
hash} with a 15-minute TTL.
Asynq
 task 
auth:send
_magic_link
 dispatches the email with the link /auth/
verify?token
=<raw>.
GET /auth/verify: API hashes the incoming token, looks up Redis, validates TTL, deletes the key (
single-use
).
If the User has no password set (magic-link-only account), email is marked verified automatically on first use.
On success: same session + token issuance pipeline as 
email+password
 login.
OAuth Flow (Google, extensible)
Standard PKCE + Authorization Code flow.
OAuth state (
code_verifier
, state param) stored in a short-lived signed cookie. No Redis involvement.
On callback: 
upsert
 User and 
oauth_accounts
 record. If a User with the same email already exists, the OAuth identity is linked to the existing account.
On success: same session + token issuance pipeline as all other auth methods.
Token Refresh Flow
Frontend calls POST /auth/refresh. The 
httpOnly
 cookie is sent automatically by the browser.
API reads the raw refresh token from the cookie, computes SHA-256, queries 
refresh_tokens
 in Postgres.
Validates: record 
found,
 
revoked_at
 IS NULL, 
expires_at
 in the future.
Revokes the old refresh token row (sets 
revoked_at
 = 
now(
)). Deletes the associated Redis session.
Creates a new Redis session and a new Postgres 
refresh_tokens
 row.
Returns new JWT access token in the response body. Sets a new 
httpOnly
 cookie with the new refresh token. Old cookie is overwritten.
JWT Payload & Middleware

Used for audit logs and debugging.
Middleware Validation Chain
Executed in order on every authenticated request:
Extract Bearer token from Authorization header. Return 401 if absent or malformed.
Verify JWT signature and exp claim. Return 401 if invalid or expired.
Extract 
session_id
 from claims. Look up 
session:{
session_id
} in Redis.
Validate: session exists, 
revoked_at
 IS NULL, 
expires_at
 not passed, 
absolute_expires_at
 not passed.
Verify SHA-256(
incoming_token
) == 
session.token
_hash
. Return 401 on mismatch (token substitution).
Slide 
session.expires
_at
 forward (capped at 
absolute_expires_at
). Update 
last_activity_at
.
Set Postgres session variable 
app.org_id
 for RLS. Inject auth context into Go request context.
Invoke role and 
plan_gate
 checks as required by the route definition.
Password Management Endpoints

bcrypt
 cost 12. Rate limits: POST /auth/login — 10 
req
/min per IP with progressive delay after 5 failures. POST /auth/register — 5 
req
/min per IP. POST /auth/magic — 5 
req
/min per IP. All auth error responses use identical timing and wording to prevent user enumeration. Password reset and email verification tokens are single-use SHA-256 hashed values in Redis.
4.2  Multi
-Tenancy & Roles
One user account can belong to multiple organisations, each with independent billing and venue collections. Roles are scoped per-organisation membership.

Cannot create, edit, or delete any records.
The active venue is stored in the JWT claim 
active_venue_id
. The frontend displays a venue switcher in the top navigation. Switching venue triggers a token refresh endpoint that reissues the JWT with the new 
active_venue_id
 — no re-authentication required.

plan_gate
 middleware reads 
subscription.plan
 from the JWT and checks a static feature flag map before allowing access to restricted endpoints. Free: 1 venue max, 50 recipes max, no analytics export. Pro: 3 venues, unlimited recipes, full analytics. Business: unlimited venues, webhook API, white-label options.
4.3  Recipe
 Costing & Margins
Recipes are the financial centre of the platform. Every recipe has an automatically computed cost derived from its ingredients' current unit prices.
Key Calculations

_qty

Cost History & Snapshots
Every change to an Ingredient's 
unit_cost
 creates a 
cost_snapshot
 record (
ingredient_id
, 
unit_cost
, 
effective_from
, source). This enables:
Trend charts: food cost % of a recipe over time, even when ingredient prices changed.
Audit trail: which electronic invoice triggered each price change, the invoice date, supplier, and line description. Manual overrides are also tracked (
unit_cost_source
 = manual) but are clearly distinguished from invoice-driven updates.
Sub-Recipe Support
A 
RecipeIngredient
 row can reference either an Ingredient (leaf) or another Recipe (sub-recipe). The cost rollup is recursive up to 3 levels. The Go domain layer detects cycles before saving and rejects circular references with a 422 
Unprocessable
 Entity response.

4.4  Inventory
 Tracking
Inventory is managed as an immutable movement log. The current stock level for any ingredient is always computed as 
SUM(
quantity_delta
) across all movements — there is no mutable "current quantity" field. This design makes the inventory fully auditable and supports retroactive corrections.

<w:tcPr><w:tcW w:w="9160" w:type="dxa"/><w:tcBorders><w:top w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:left w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:bottom w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:right w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/></w:tcBorders><w:shd w:val="clear" w:color="auto" w:fill="FDF0EB"/><w:tcMar><w:top w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:left w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/><w:bottom w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:right w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/></w:tcMar><w:vAlign w:val="center"/></w:tcPr><w:p w14:paraId="3C599397" w14:textId="77777777" w:rsidR="00814D14" w:rsidRDefault="00000000"><w:pPr><w:spacing w:line="300" w:lineRule="auto"/></w:pPr><w:r><w:rPr><w:color w:val="222222"/><w:sz w:val="21"/><w:szCs w:val="21"/></w:rPr><w:t>Purchase stock movements (type = purchase) are created exclusively by the electronic invoice import pipeline. There is no manual "mark as received" button on a PO. Stock is only incremented when a supplier's electronic invoice is uploaded, parsed, matched to ingredients, and confirmed by the user. Theoretical vs Actual Variance
For venues that record portions sold (manual input or CSV import), 
FoodCost
 computes:
Theoretical usage = 
SUM(
recipe_
ingredient.quantity
 × 
portions_sold
) per ingredient over the period.
Actual usage = 
SUM(
usage + waste movements) for the same period.
Variance % = (actual − theoretical) ÷ theoretical × 100.
Positive variance indicates over-use or unrecorded waste. This is one of the most actionable metrics in the platform.
Low Stock Alerts
When an 
InventoryMovement
 is written that brings current stock below an ingredient's 
reorder_threshold
, the Go handler enqueues an 
Asynq
 task 
inventory:low
_stock_alert
. The worker sends an email and, if configured, fires 
an
 outbound webhook event 
inventory.low_stock
.
4.5  Supplier
 & Purchase Order Management
The Supplier module manages vendor relationships and purchase orders. Purchase orders are optional planning tools — they can be created to track expected deliveries and matched against incoming invoices, but they are not required. Inventory stock and ingredient costs are updated exclusively when a supplier's electronic invoice is uploaded and confirmed.
Purchase Order Lifecycle

No inventory or cost effect. Reason field required.
Invoice-to-PO Matching (optional)
When an electronic invoice is uploaded, the system attempts to auto-match it to an open PO by supplier and approximate date. If a match is found with high confidence, the PO is advanced to 
invoice_received
 automatically. If no PO exists, the invoice can be processed standalone — no PO is required for stock and cost updates.
Automatic Cost Updates on Invoice Confirmation
When the user confirms the ingredient matching from an electronic invoice, the Go domain layer:
Creates one 
InventoryMovement
 of type purchase per matched line (
quantity_delta
 = invoice line quantity, 
cost_at_time
 = invoice unit price, 
source_invoice_line_id
 = FK to the invoice line).
Updates each matched 
Ingredient.unit_cost
 using an Exponentially Weighted Moving Average (EWMA) with α = 0.3 by default (configurable per venue). This smooths price spikes while tracking drift.
Writes a 
cost_snapshot
 record for every Ingredient whose 
unit_cost
 changed (used for trend charts and audit trail).
If linked to a PO, advances the PO status to matched and then immediately to closed.
4.6  Menu
 Engineering & Pricing Suggestions
Menu engineering applies the classic BCG-style matrix analysis to menu items, combining profitability (contribution margin) and popularity (portions sold) to classify each item and surface actionable recommendations.

4.7  Analytics
 & Reporting
All analytics in v1 are computed queries against PostgreSQL. No separate OLAP store is required at v1 scale. Each report endpoint accepts optional date range parameters (from, to) and 
a ?format
=csv query parameter for exports.

Export Behaviour
Small exports (< 5,000 rows): synchronous streaming CSV response.
Large exports (≥ 5,000 rows): asynchronous — 
Asynq
 task 
csv:export
_large
. User receives an email with a pre-signed download link when the file is ready.
All exports are scoped to the active venue and authenticated org. Download links are pre-signed with a 24-hour expiry.

All Stripe webhook requests are verified with 
stripe.ConstructEvent
 using the endpoint's signing secret before any processing occurs. Invalid signatures return 400 and are logged. The Stripe Billing Portal is used for self-serve plan changes — no custom billing UI is required.
4.9  Outbound
 Webhooks
Business-tier organisations can register webhook endpoints to receive real-time events from 
FoodCost
. This enables integration with POS systems, accounting software, and internal tooling.

Delivery Mechanism
On event: Go handler enqueues an 
Asynq
 task 
webhook:dispatch
 with the payload and target endpoint IDs.
Worker executes the HTTP POST with a maximum 10-second timeout.
Payload is signed with HMAC-SHA256 using the endpoint's secret. Signature delivered in X-
FoodCost
-Signature header.
Retry schedule: 3 attempts with exponential backoff — 1 minute, 5 minutes, 30 minutes.
Every attempt is logged to the 
webhook_delivery
 table regardless of outcome.
After 3 failed attempts, the endpoint is marked inactive and the org owner is notified.
4.10  Electronic
 Invoice Import
The electronic invoice import pipeline is the single and exclusive way to update purchase prices and increment inventory stock. Suppliers upload their invoices in a standard electronic format (
FatturaPA
 for Italian suppliers, UBL or CII for international), and the system parses, matches, and confirms the data automatically.

Supported for completeness.
Upload & Parsing Flow
User uploads one or more invoice XML files via POST /venues
/:vid
/invoices (multipart, max 10 MB per file, max 20 files per request).
API validates file size and MIME type (application/xml or text/xml). Returns 202 Accepted with a list of 
invoice_ids
 and an 
import_job_id
.
Asynq
 task 
invoice:parse
 is enqueued per file. Worker detects format (
FatturaPA
 vs UBL vs CII by root element), parses XML into typed structs, extracts all line items, and persists 
ElectronicInvoice
 + 
ElectronicInvoiceLine
 rows with status = parsed.
Worker attempts supplier auto-
match:
 looks up Supplier by SDI code (
FatturaPA
) or VAT number. If matched, sets 
invoice.supplier
_id
. If no supplier found, the invoice is flagged for manual supplier assignment.
Worker attempts PO auto-
match:
 finds open POs for the matched supplier with invoice date within ±7 days of PO 
expected_at
. If found with high confidence, links invoice to PO (
matched_po_id
) and advances PO status to 
invoice_received
.
User receives a notification (email + in-app) that invoices are ready for review.
Ingredient Matching & Confirmation UI
After parsing, the user is presented with a matching review screen for each invoice. This is the only interactive step before stock and costs are updated.

/invoices/:id/confirm. Locked until all lines are either matched or explicitly skipped.

Skipped lines generate no movement and no cost update.
Confirmation Pipeline (POST /venues
/:vid
/invoices/:id/confirm)
API validates all lines are matched or skipped. Returns 422 if any unresolved lines remain.
Enqueues 
Asynq
 task 
invoice:import
 with the confirmed mapping. Returns 202 Accepted.
Worker runs the full confirmation pipeline inside a single Postgres transaction:
Creates one 
InventoryMovement
 (type = purchase) per confirmed line.
Updates 
Ingredient.unit_cost
 via EWMA for each matched ingredient.
Writes a 
cost_snapshot
 record per changed ingredient.
Advances 
ElectronicInvoice.status
 to imported.
If linked to a PO: advances PO to matched, then to closed.
On 
success:
 notifies user by email with a summary (N ingredients updated, N movements created, total invoice value, any skipped lines).
On transaction failure: status set to error, error detail stored in 
ElectronicInvoice.error_detail
 (JSONB). User notified. No partial updates.
Invoice History & Audit
All raw XML is stored in 
ElectronicInvoice.raw_xml
. Nothing is ever deleted.
Every 
InventoryMovement
 of type purchase carries 
source_invoice_line_id
 — a permanent link from a stock change to the exact invoice line that caused it.
The frontend invoice history page allows filtering by supplier, date range, and status. Each invoice can be expanded to show all lines, their matched ingredients, and the resulting movements.
Analytics report: Supplier price drift now sources exclusively from invoice-confirmed 
cost_snapshot
 records, giving exact per-delivery price history.
ElectronicInvoice
 Entity — Key Fields

Stored for analytics.
4.11  CSV
 Import / Export
CSV import is used for master data and manual stock counts. It does not create purchase movements or update ingredient costs. Those operations are exclusively handled by the electronic invoice pipeline (section 4.10).

Import Processing
User uploads CSV via POST /venues
/:vid
/import (multipart form).
API validates file size (max 50 MB) and MIME type, then enqueues 
Asynq
 task 
csv:import
. Returns 202 Accepted with 
job_id
.
Worker processes rows in batches of 500. Each row is validated individually.
Errors are collected per-row and written to 
import_
jobs.error
_detail
 (JSONB). The import does not stop on row errors.
On completion, the job status is 
updated
 and the user is notified by email with a summary (N rows imported, M errors).
GET /import-jobs/:id returns 
current status
 and progress for polling or 
websocket
 push.

Port 8081. Connects to Redis.
One Binary, Two Roles
The Go binary accepts a -mode flag at startup. Both the API server and the worker are compiled into the same binary and share all domain packages:

app -mode 
api
Starts the net/http server on :8080. Registers all route handlers. Connects to Postgres and Redis. Runs 
golang
-migrate on startup.

app -mode worker
Starts the 
Asynq
 server. Registers all task handlers. Connects to Postgres (for write-backs) and Redis (queue broker). No HTTP server.
Volume & Secret Management
All secrets are passed via environment variables, loaded from a .env file (never committed to version control).
Postgres and Redis use named volumes (
pgdata
, 
redisdata
) for persistence across container restarts.
The .
env
.example
 file documents every required variable with placeholder values and is 
version-controlled
.
In production, secrets should be injected via the host environment or a secrets manager (e.g. Docker Secrets, AWS SSM). 
The .env
 approach is for local development only.

The Go codebase follows a strict layered architecture. Dependency arrows point inward only: handlers depend on domain, domain depends on nothing outside the standard library, stores implement domain interfaces.

Called only from worker tasks.
Handler Pattern
Every HTTP handler follows the same structure to keep the codebase consistent and easy to navigate. Business logic is never in the handler layer:
Decode and validate the request body using a typed input struct.
Extract authenticated context (
user_id
, 
org_id
, 
venue_id
, role) from request context.
Call the relevant domain function with typed parameters. Domain function returns a typed result or a domain error.
If the operation requires a database side effect, call the store repository.
Map domain errors to HTTP status codes (e.g. 
ErrNotFound
 → 404, 
ErrForbidden
 → 403, 
ErrPlanLimit
 → 402).
Write the JSON response with encoding/
json
.

 Workers — Task Catalogue
Asynq
 is used for five categories of work that must not block HTTP request handlers. The worker process runs as a separate container (same binary, -mode worker flag) and connects to the Redis queue broker and PostgreSQL for write-backs.

The following three architectural decisions cannot be made without additional input. They each have schema or API design implications that will affect multiple modules if changed after development begins.

portions_sold
 per menu item. The 
MenuItem.popularity_score
 field can be populated by: (1) manual entry per item per period, (2) CSV import on the existing import flow, or (3) a future POS webhook integration. The choice affects whether we need a 
SalesRecord
 entity, how we handle time periods for the matrix, and whether the matrix is a point-in-time snapshot or a rolling window.
Decision B — Free Tier Plan Gate Behaviour

<w:tcPr><w:tcW w:w="9160" w:type="dxa"/><w:tcBorders><w:top w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:left w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:bottom w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:right w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/></w:tcBorders><w:shd w:val="clear" w:color="auto" w:fill="FDF0EB"/><w:tcMar><w:top w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:left w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/><w:bottom w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:right w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/></w:tcMar><w:vAlign w:val="center"/></w:tcPr><w:p w14:paraId="450C1D45" w14:textId="77777777" w:rsidR="00814D14" w:rsidRDefault="00000000"><w:pPr><w:spacing w:line="300" w:lineRule="auto"/></w:pPr><w:r><w:rPr><w:color w:val="222222"/><w:sz w:val="21"/><w:szCs w:val="21"/></w:rPr><w:t xml:space="preserve">When a Free-tier user attempts to exceed their plan limits (e.g. adding a second venue, or creating recipe #51), the system can 
either:
 (A) Hard block — return 403 Forbidden with an upgrade prompt. The venue/recipe is never created. (B) Soft lock — create the record but disable access/display until the user upgrades. Option A is simpler to implement and reason about. Option B allows a smoother trial experience. This decision affects the 
plan_gate
 middleware design and the frontend upgrade flow.
Decision C — Sub-Recipe Recursion Depth

<w:tcPr><w:tcW w:w="9160" w:type="dxa"/><w:tcBorders><w:top w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:left w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:bottom w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/><w:right w:val="none" w:sz="0" w:space="0" w:color="FFFFFF"/></w:tcBorders><w:shd w:val="clear" w:color="auto" w:fill="FDF0EB"/><w:tcMar><w:top w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:left w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/><w:bottom w:w="100" w:type="dxa"/><w:right w:w="140" w:type="dxa"/></w:tcMar><w:vAlign w:val="center"/></w:tcPr><w:p w14:paraId="172496ED" w14:textId="77777777" w:rsidR="00814D14" w:rsidRDefault="00000000"><w:pPr><w:spacing w:line="300" w:lineRule="auto"/></w:pPr><w:r><w:rPr><w:color w:val="222222"/><w:sz w:val="21"/><w:szCs w:val="21"/></w:rPr><w:t xml:space="preserve">Sub-recipes (a recipe that uses another recipe as an ingredient) require recursive cost rollup. The current design proposes a practical cap of 3 levels to keep query complexity manageable. If unlimited depth is required, the Go domain layer needs cycle detection (DFS with a visited set) and the Postgres cost query 
needs
 a recursive CTE. Confirm the maximum nesting level before implementing the 
RecipeIngredient
 store layer.
